subject of the roads, he would encourage people to keep the roads in a 
good state in their villages instead of intentionally creating bogholes.
 He explained that in the short term they would loose 'income', but it 
would benefit them in the longer term if traffic increased (more and 
thus cheaper supplies, an actual functional economy).<br>
<br>
It was very refreshing to hear this and we really hoped his talk would 
make an impression. When his talk was finished we saw the people leaving
 the mission. 4 man had attended. <br>
<br>
That evening Omer had invited us to join them for dinner. We reluctantly
 accepted. Eating with 'the locals' is always in interesting experience,
 but very often it is just very bad food. As to not offend people and 
also as a precaution for us not to get sick, we tried to avoid these 
offerings in Congo as much a possible. We could see most people were 
happy when we declined as they have so little food already.<br>
<br>
We were pleasantly surprised, maniok leaves, something that resembled a 
stew and fufu (= pap for the South Africans, but made out of maniok).<br>
<br>


	
	
		
		
	


	<div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-3-1587032636" data-lb-id="_xfUid-3-1587032636">
		
			<div class="lbContainer-zoomer js-lbImage-_xfUid-3-1587032636" data-src="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo032.jpg" aria-label="Zoom"></div>
		
		<img src="congo_files/kabombo032.jpg" data-url="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo032.jpg" class="bbImage" data-zoom-target="1" alt="" style="">
	</div></div><hr><div class="bbWrapper">This is Josephine's diary. It is
 not overly detailed but is great to recollect some 'lost' memories. It 
is the base of this trip report.<br>
<br>


	
	
		
		
	


	<div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-4-1587032636" data-lb-id="_xfUid-4-1587032636">
		
			<div class="lbContainer-zoomer js-lbImage-_xfUid-4-1587032636" data-src="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/dagboek012.jpg" aria-label="Zoom"></div>
		
		<img src="congo_files/dagboek012.jpg" data-url="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/dagboek012.jpg" class="bbImage" data-zoom-target="1" alt="" style="">
	</div>

<br>
<br>
She made this illustration from the road to Ilebo<br>
<br>


	
	
		
		
	


	<div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-5-1587032636" data-lb-id="_xfUid-5-1587032636">
		
			<div class="lbContainer-zoomer js-lbImage-_xfUid-5-1587032636" data-src="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/dagboek002.jpg" aria-label="Zoom"></div>
		
		<img src="congo_files/dagboek002.jpg" data-url="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/dagboek002.jpg" class="bbImage" data-zoom-target="1" alt="" style="">
	</div></div><hr><div class="bbWrapper"><b>Day 19</b><br>
<br>
Our car was packed and ready to go... we were waiting for a phone call 
from Barthélémy. When it finally arrived around noon, Abbé Omer told us 
he had notified the police that we would be leaving from the port today.
 We have no idea why he did that, as he knew how much trouble we've had 
with the police thus far.<br>
<br>
Anyway, on his little motorbike he guided us towards the port. Not 
surprisingly we were stopped by the police who were waiting for us. As 
our boat was waiting for us, we were not in the mood for a lengthy 
negotiation. So as soon as they had checked all our papers (our self 
issued 'permit' was still doing wonders) we played bluff and immediately
 asked for their names and ranks and claimed we knew their superior 
officer. This was a risky move and probably a stupid risk to take at the
 time, but it worked. Somehow we sensed by now how confident the police 
was and we could play the game along quite well.<br>
<br>
We couldn't believe our eyes when we finally saw the ferry.<br>
<br>


	
	
		
		
	


	<div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-6-1587032636" data-lb-id="_xfUid-6-1587032636">
		
			<div class="lbContainer-zoomer js-lbImage-_xfUid-6-1587032636" data-src="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo042.jpg" aria-label="Zoom"></div>
		
		<img src="congo_files/kabombo042.jpg" data-url="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo042.jpg" class="bbImage" data-zoom-target="1" alt="" style="">
	</div>

<br>
<br>
It looked brand new! <br>
It wasn't..<br>
<br>
A German (?) NGO had funded the restoration of the ferry recently. It 
had received a nice fresh coat of paint, but the money to rebuild the 
engines had gone missing.<br>
<br>


	
	
		
		
	


	<div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-7-1587032636" data-lb-id="_xfUid-7-1587032636">
		
			<div class="lbContainer-zoomer js-lbImage-_xfUid-7-1587032636" data-src="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo041.jpg" aria-label="Zoom"></div>
		
		<img src="congo_files/kabombo041.jpg" data-url="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo041.jpg" class="bbImage" data-zoom-target="1" alt="" style="">
	</div></div><hr><div class="bbWrapper">The departure in the port of 
Ilebo was hectic (dodgy place!) so we could not really say goodbye to 
Abbé Omer. He waved us goodbye from the shore. I did not get along well 
with Omer. I cannot explain why. But in retrospect he did help us and my
 lack of trust in him was probably a mistake. Shame..<br>
<br>


	
	
		
		
	


	<div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-8-1587032636" data-lb-id="_xfUid-8-1587032636">
		
			<div class="lbContainer-zoomer js-lbImage-_xfUid-8-1587032636" data-src="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo033.jpg" aria-label="Zoom"></div>
		
		<img src="congo_files/kabombo033.jpg" data-url="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo033.jpg" class="bbImage" data-zoom-target="1" alt="" style="">
	</div>

<br>
Abbé Omer on the right.<br>
<br>
The trip across would take an hour as it was upstream. We felt very much
 at ease during that hour. We were disconnected from the shore, on a 
safe distance from everybody that wanted money from us, we did not have 
to drive. We just had to sit back and enjoy the ride. It was also one of
 the rare occasions were we got out of the dense forest and could have a
 look around.<br>
<br>


	
	
		
		
	


	<div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-9-1587032636" data-lb-id="_xfUid-9-1587032636">
		
			<div class="lbContainer-zoomer js-lbImage-_xfUid-9-1587032636" data-src="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo038.jpg" aria-label="Zoom"></div>
		
		<img src="congo_files/kabombo038.jpg" data-url="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo038.jpg" class="bbImage" data-zoom-target="1" alt="" style="">
	</div>

<br>
<br>


	
	
		
		
	


	<div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-10-1587032636" data-lb-id="_xfUid-10-1587032636">
		
			<div class="lbContainer-zoomer js-lbImage-_xfUid-10-1587032636" data-src="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo045.jpg" aria-label="Zoom"></div>
		
		<img src="congo_files/kabombo045.jpg" data-url="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo045.jpg" class="bbImage" data-zoom-target="1" alt="" style="">
	</div>

<br>
<br>
The port of Ilebo<br>
<br>


	
	
		
		
	


	<div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-11-1587032636" data-lb-id="_xfUid-11-1587032636">
		
			<div class="lbContainer-zoomer js-lbImage-_xfUid-11-1587032636" data-src="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo048.jpg" aria-label="Zoom"></div>
		
		<img src="congo_files/kabombo048.jpg" data-url="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo048.jpg" class="bbImage" data-zoom-target="1" alt="" style="">
	</div>

<br>
<br>


	
	
		
		
	


	<div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-12-1587032636" data-lb-id="_xfUid-12-1587032636">
		
			<div class="lbContainer-zoomer js-lbImage-_xfUid-12-1587032636" data-src="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo053.jpg" aria-label="Zoom"></div>
		
		<img src="congo_files/kabombo053.jpg" data-url="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo053.jpg" class="bbImage" data-zoom-target="1" alt="" style="">
	</div>

<br>
<br>
A floating fishing village on the shores of the Kasai:<br>
<br>


	
	
		
		
	


	<div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-13-1587032636" data-lb-id="_xfUid-13-1587032636">
		
			<div class="lbContainer-zoomer js-lbImage-_xfUid-13-1587032636" data-src="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo050.jpg" aria-label="Zoom"></div>
		
		<img src="congo_files/kabombo050.jpg" data-url="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo050.jpg" class="bbImage" data-zoom-target="1" alt="" style="">
	</div></div><hr><div class="bbWrapper">The boat crew:<br>
<br>
Barthélémy<br>
<br>


	
	
		
		
	


	<div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-14-1587032636" data-lb-id="_xfUid-14-1587032636">
		
			<div class="lbContainer-zoomer js-lbImage-_xfUid-14-1587032636" data-src="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo047.jpg" aria-label="Zoom"></div>

	</div>

<br>
<br>
The captain<br>
<br>


	
	
		
		
	


	<div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-15-1587032636" data-lb-id="_xfUid-15-1587032636">

	</div>

<br>
<br>
Once out of the port these two guys would use long sticks to feel how the deep the river was.<br>
<br>


	
	
		
		
	


	<div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-16-1587032636" data-lb-id="_xfUid-16-1587032636">
		
			<div class="lbContainer-zoomer js-lbImage-_xfUid-16-1587032636" data-src="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo046.jpg" aria-label="Zoom"></div>

	</div>

<br>
<br>
The mechanics<br>
<br>


	
	
		
		
	


	<div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-17-1587032636" data-lb-id="_xfUid-17-1587032636">
		
			<div class="lbContainer-zoomer js-lbImage-_xfUid-17-1587032636" data-src="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo062.jpg" aria-label="Zoom"></div>
		
		<img src="congo_files/kabombo062.jpg" data-url="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo062.jpg" class="bbImage" data-zoom-target="1" alt="" style="">
	</div>

<br>
<br>
This dugout canoe hitched a ride with us. For a moment I thought of 
charging them a fee... according to local Congolese tradition. I didn't 
;-)<br>
<br>


	
	
		
		
	


	<div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-18-1587032636" data-lb-id="_xfUid-18-1587032636">

	</div>

	<div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-19-1587032636" data-lb-id="_xfUid-19-1587032636">

	<div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-20-1587032636" data-lb-id="_xfUid-20-1587032636">

	</div>

<br>
<br>
The beautiful, mighty, muddy Kasai river<br>
<br>


	
	
		
		
	


	<div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-21-1587032636" data-lb-id="_xfUid-21-1587032636">
		
			<div class="lbContainer-zoomer js-lbImage-_xfUid-21-1587032636" data-src="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo063.jpg" aria-label="Zoom"></div>
		
		<img src="congo_files/kabombo063.jpg" data-url="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo063.jpg" class="bbImage" data-zoom-target="1" alt="" style="">
	</div>

<br>
<br>


	
	
		
		
	


	<div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-22-1587032636" data-lb-id="_xfUid-22-1587032636">
		
			<div class="lbContainer-zoomer js-lbImage-_xfUid-22-1587032636" data-src="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo065.jpg" aria-label="Zoom"></div>
		
		<img src="congo_files/kabombo065.jpg" data-url="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo065.jpg" class="bbImage" data-zoom-target="1" alt="" style="">
	</div>

<br>
-<br>
<br>


	
	
		
		
	


	<div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-23-1587032636" data-lb-id="_xfUid-23-1587032636">
		
			<div class="lbContainer-zoomer js-lbImage-_xfUid-23-1587032636" data-src="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo058.jpg" aria-label="Zoom"></div>
		
		<img src="congo_files/kabombo058.jpg" data-url="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo058.jpg" class="bbImage" data-zoom-target="1" alt="" style="">
	</div>

<br>
<br>


	
	
		
		
	


	<div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-24-1587032636" data-lb-id="_xfUid-24-1587032636">
		
			<div class="lbContainer-zoomer js-lbImage-_xfUid-24-1587032636" data-src="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo067.jpg" aria-label="Zoom"></div>
		
		<img src="congo_files/kabombo067.jpg" data-url="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo067.jpg" class="bbImage" data-zoom-target="1" alt="" style="">
	</div>

<br>
<br>
In Ilebo we had asked for the road conditions on the other side of the 
river. Nobody knew. People here travel by boat, not by road. We were 
silently hoping that the roads would be perfect on the other side. We 
couldn't have been more wrong...<br>
<br>
Barthélémy gave us a letter for his friend, the captain of the next 
ferry. It was written in Lingala, but we could make out a few words. The
 letter was about us but we did not know what it said. Would it be some 
good words for us? Or would it be some tips on how to extort the maximum
 amount of money out of us? We did not ask about the condition of the 
next ferry... It was probably better that we didn't know at the time..</div><hr><div class="bbWrapper">Thanks
 for all the great feedback guys. Keep it coming! It's amazing to see 
how many people are following us on this little trip!<br>
<br>
Stay tuned.. we are not there yet ;-)</div><hr><div class="bbWrapper">The
 'jetty' on the opposite side of the Kasai river used to have a nice 
concrete ramp, but it had long disintegrated. To get of the boat we were
 litterally dropped on the muddy shore. Scary!<br>
<br>
There was a little village here, and our hopes for better roads vanished as soon we left the village.<br>
<br>


	
	
		
		
	


	<div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-1-1587032647" data-lb-id="_xfUid-1-1587032647">
		
			<div class="lbContainer-zoomer js-lbImage-_xfUid-1-1587032647" data-src="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo068.jpg" aria-label="Zoom"></div>
		
		<img src="congo_files/kabombo068.jpg" data-url="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo068.jpg" class="bbImage" data-zoom-target="1" alt="" style="">
	</div>

<br>
<br>
The road was no more then a tight single track. It is barely possible to
 pass trough here with a bicycle. After having verified that this is 
indeed the only track leading out of here we had no choice but to drive 
trough it. Half of the car was cuting trough the bushes. The amount of 
spiders and other critters that we took along for a ride was staggering.
 It was impossible to keep the windows open because of the debri that 
was coming in. It was also impossible to keep the windows closed because
 it was so hot.<br>
<br>


	
	
		
		
	


	<div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-2-1587032647" data-lb-id="_xfUid-2-1587032647">
		
			<div class="lbContainer-zoomer js-lbImage-_xfUid-2-1587032647" data-src="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo069.jpg" aria-label="Zoom"></div>
		
		<img src="congo_files/kabombo069.jpg" data-url="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo069.jpg" class="bbImage" data-zoom-target="1" alt="" style="">
	</div>

<br>
<br>


	
	
		
		
	


	<div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-3-1587032647" data-lb-id="_xfUid-3-1587032647">
		
			<div class="lbContainer-zoomer js-lbImage-_xfUid-3-1587032647" data-src="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo070.jpg" aria-label="Zoom"></div>
		
		<img src="congo_files/kabombo070.jpg" data-url="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo070.jpg" class="bbImage" data-zoom-target="1" alt="" style="">
	</div>

<br>
<br>
This went on for kilometers on end. Spiders were atually building a web on the inside of our windscreen. <br>
<br>
And then we tipped over again.<br>
<br>


	
	
		
		
	


	<div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-4-1587032647" data-lb-id="_xfUid-4-1587032647">
		
			<div class="lbContainer-zoomer js-lbImage-_xfUid-4-1587032647" data-src="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo097.jpg" aria-label="Zoom"></div>
		
		<img src="congo_files/kabombo097.jpg" data-url="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo097.jpg" class="bbImage" data-zoom-target="1" alt="" style="">
	</div></div><hr><div class="bbWrapper"><blockquote class="bbCodeBlock bbCodeBlock--expandable bbCodeBlock--quote">
	
		<div class="bbCodeBlock-title">
			
				<a href="file:///forum/goto/post?id=742677" class="bbCodeBlock-sourceJump" data-xf-click="attribution" data-content-selector="#post-742677">Area56 said:</a>
			
		</div>
	
	<div class="bbCodeBlock-content">
		
		<div class="bbCodeBlock-expandContent ">
			but I have to wonder how you could plan for such a trip without considering this type of situation.
		</div>
		<div class="bbCodeBlock-expandLink"><a>Click to expand...</a></div>
	</div>
</blockquote>I can see your point... but... ;-)<br>
<br>
How many situations can one foresee? You could apply the same logic to 
almost anything. What about medical supplies? We carried quite an 
extensive kit, but if Josephine got bitten by that snake, what do we do?
 What about spare parts? What if the gearbox dies? (it did actually, in 
South Africa). In both case: seek help locally is the answer.<br>
<br>
What if your house collapses when you are sitting at your desk? ;-)<br>
<br>
That does not mean one should be reckless. And I dare say we are 
actually pretty prudent. It might not tell from this report, but we 
avoid unnecessary risks if at all possible. But that risk assessment is a
 personal thing ofcourse.<br>
<br>
Oh, and we do carry a hi-lift, we have used it extensively for our recoveries in Congo (sorry for not making that clear <img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" class="smilie smilie--sprite smilie--sprite1" alt=":)" title="Smile    :)" data-shortname=":)">
 ). The hi-lift combined with the sand ladders and a bit of digging 
always did the trick. An additional bottle jack can do magic things too.
 We are also familiar with using a hi-lift as a pulling device. But that
 is such a horrible thing to do that we avoid it if at all possible.<br>
<br>
Oh, and we did crash trough a bridge once. We got out of that one with 
our hi-lift, sand ladders and a lot of patience and creativity.<br>
<br>
And then there is the danger of the human mind. We do not have a winch, 
so we drive with caution, making sure that werever we get ourselves 
stuck in, we can get out again. <br>
If I'd have a winch, I know I'd be much quicker in just risking it, 
knowing that I can winch myself out if it doesn't work. And then the 
winch stops working...<br>
The fewer of these tools you have, the more you are obliged to think about your next step. And that is not a bad thing! ;-)<br>
<br>
That said, a winch is on my shopping list, but it is not a priority.</div><hr><div class="bbWrapper">The
 single track trough the forest went on forever. We had 'lost' a lot of 
time by taking the ferry and before we knew it got dark. We were still 
driving with one wheel on the track and the other in the forest. The 
darkness made it quite spooky. In the eroded bits the darkness made it 
very difficult to manouver our way trough. The mosquitoes were feasting 
on our blood.<br>
<br>
We passed a few villages in the forest, but they looked very primitive 
and wild. Not the most welcoming environment. We decided to push on 
until the village of Basongo, which looked big on our map.<br>
<br>
It wasn't.<br>
<br>
There was no mission here, they also did not have a priest, or a father,
 or .. . But they did have a "Maison de passage". A little hut where 
people who'd pass trough could sleep. We could camp next to this house.<br>
<br>
The crowd was huge and the kids went completely berzerk as soon as we 
stopped in the village. When driving to the maison de passage a dozen or
 so kids (and a few adults) jumped on our wheelcarrier at the back. 
Josephine had to walk behind the car to keep them off.<br>
<br>
This would be a zero-privacy night again. It didn't matter much as it was half past 10 already and we just wanted to sleep. <br>
<br>
With the crowd around, it seemed as if going for a pee before going to bed was the biggest challenge of the day.</div><hr><div class="bbWrapper">Progress on day 19. Basongo<br>
<br>


	
	
		
		
	


	<div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-2-1587032658" data-lb-id="_xfUid-2-1587032658">
		
			<div class="lbContainer-zoomer js-lbImage-_xfUid-2-1587032658" data-src="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/congo_democratic_republic_map-day19.jpg" aria-label="Zoom"></div>
		
		<img src="congo_files/congo_democratic_republic_map-day19.jpg" data-url="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/congo_democratic_republic_map-day19.jpg" class="bbImage" data-zoom-target="1" alt="" style="">
	</div></div><hr><div class="bbWrapper"><b>Day 20</b><br>
<br>
Busy villages are not a good place to sleep late. We left Basongo at 5.30.<br>
Eventually the road opened up. <br>
<br>


	
	
		
		
	


	<div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-3-1587032658" data-lb-id="_xfUid-3-1587032658">
		
			<div class="lbContainer-zoomer js-lbImage-_xfUid-3-1587032658" data-src="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo072.jpg" aria-label="Zoom"></div>
		
		<img src="congo_files/kabombo072.jpg" data-url="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo072.jpg" class="bbImage" data-zoom-target="1" alt="" style="">
	</div>

<br>
<br>


	
	
		
		
	


	<div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-4-1587032658" data-lb-id="_xfUid-4-1587032658">
		
			<div class="lbContainer-zoomer js-lbImage-_xfUid-4-1587032658" data-src="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo074.jpg" aria-label="Zoom"></div>
		
		<img src="congo_files/kabombo074.jpg" data-url="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo074.jpg" class="bbImage" data-zoom-target="1" alt="" style="">
	</div>

<br>
<br>
We were on top of a hill, and we had to find ourselves a way trough the 
valley to the next hill. The deeper we descended into the valley, the 
worse the erosion got. Troughout the years about 10 parallel roads - a 
few kilometers apart - had been made down the hill. If the road got too 
bad, they just created a new tracks. The soil was sandy. We had to drive
 10 km's to find the latest track<br>
<br>


	
	
		
		
	


	<div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-5-1587032658" data-lb-id="_xfUid-5-1587032658">
		
			<div class="lbContainer-zoomer js-lbImage-_xfUid-5-1587032658" data-src="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo076.jpg" aria-label="Zoom"></div>
		
		<img src="congo_files/kabombo076.jpg" data-url="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo076.jpg" class="bbImage" data-zoom-target="1" alt="" style="">
	</div>

<br>
<br>
But that track too soon became impossible. We had to create our own 
track trough the high grass. Josephine would walk in front and I tried 
not to loose her out of sight.<br>
<br>


	
	
		
		
	


	<div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-6-1587032658" data-lb-id="_xfUid-6-1587032658">
		
			<div class="lbContainer-zoomer js-lbImage-_xfUid-6-1587032658" data-src="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo077.jpg" aria-label="Zoom"></div>
		
		<img src="congo_files/kabombo077.jpg" data-url="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo077.jpg" class="bbImage" data-zoom-target="1" alt="" style="">
	</div>

<br>
<br>


	
	
		
		
	


	<div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-7-1587032658" data-lb-id="_xfUid-7-1587032658">
		
			<div class="lbContainer-zoomer js-lbImage-_xfUid-7-1587032658" data-src="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo078.jpg" aria-label="Zoom"></div>
		
		<img src="congo_files/kabombo078.jpg" data-url="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo078.jpg" class="bbImage" data-zoom-target="1" alt="" style="">
	</div>

<br>
<br>
In the valley was a shallow stream. We drove trough the water instead of using this bridge in case you were wondering.<br>
<br>


	
	
		
		
	


	<div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-8-1587032658" data-lb-id="_xfUid-8-1587032658">
		
			<div class="lbContainer-zoomer js-lbImage-_xfUid-8-1587032658" data-src="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo081.jpg" aria-label="Zoom"></div>
		
		<img src="congo_files/kabombo081.jpg" data-url="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo081.jpg" class="bbImage" data-zoom-target="1" alt="" style="">
	</div>

<br>
<br>
The soil on the other side of the river was more firm and for a short while we could advance quickly in a beautiful scenery!<br>
<br>


	
	
		
		
	


	<div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-9-1587032658" data-lb-id="_xfUid-9-1587032658">
		
			<div class="lbContainer-zoomer js-lbImage-_xfUid-9-1587032658" data-src="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo083.jpg" aria-label="Zoom"></div>
		
		<img src="congo_files/kabombo083.jpg" data-url="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo083.jpg" class="bbImage" data-zoom-target="1" alt="" style="">
	</div>

<br>
<br>


	
	
		
		
	


	<div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-10-1587032658" data-lb-id="_xfUid-10-1587032658">
		
			<div class="lbContainer-zoomer js-lbImage-_xfUid-10-1587032658" data-src="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo086.jpg" aria-label="Zoom"></div>
		
		<img src="congo_files/kabombo086.jpg" data-url="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo086.jpg" class="bbImage" data-zoom-target="1" alt="" style="">
	</div>

<br>
<br>
This short stretch was some of the more pleasant parts of the trip thus 
far. And open savannah where we could actually see something. Reasonable
 sandy tracks. We did get stuck a few times in the deeper ruts, but 
nothng dramatic.<br>
<br>
And most important: the people seemed to be more friendly this side of the Kasai river. We hadn't met any police yet either.</div><hr><div class="bbWrapper">With a bit of luck we'd be able to cross the Loange river today!<br>
<br>
The loange river was the second and last ferry we would have to take. 
Barthélémy in Ilebo said the ferry was functional, so that is good news 
as it was the last possible showstopper for us. On top of that, The 
Loange river is the border of the Kasai province and the Bandundu 
province. For us that was a major milestone, as Bandundu is the last 
province we would have to cross. Kinshasa is in Bandundu! It was still a
 very long way, but a milestone nonetheless.. <img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" class="smilie smilie--sprite smilie--sprite1" alt=":)" title="Smile    :)" data-shortname=":)"><br>
<br>
With a bit of luck...<br>
<br>
Could it be that luck just doesn't work in Congo? The people here never 
really had any luck with the people that ruled them. They didn't have 
much luck with the foreign relations. Some of the worlds biggest and 
most dangerous diseases (HIV, Ebola, ..) originated here... that's not 
really what I'd call luck either. You could say that they are lucky to 
have all those diamonds, gold and whatnot burried under their very 
fertile ground... But that is exactly the reason that the country has 
been in a constant state of war for the last decades. Tough luck!<br>
<br>
We would not make it to the ferry today.<br>
<br>
Where the road descended down to the river, the Savannah abruptly 
stopped and turned into dense forest. The water had eroded a deep and 
very rough track here, we were very happy to have to do this downhill so
 gravity could help us down. At the last village people had warned us 
not to start the descent as a truck was coming up in the other 
direction. It is ofcourse impossible to pass eachother there.<br>
<br>
So we stopped on top of the hill and walked down. Sure enough, a big 4x4
 truck was stuck in the tracks. They were carrying a GSM tower that was 
to be constructed on the savannah behind us. They had hired a team of 
about 20 man to assist the truck on this stretch. Armed with shovels and
 pickaxes they more-or-less levelled the road. We watched them for an 
hour and they had moved about 10 meter in that time. It was another 
kilometer to the top. This would take some time!<br>
<br>
So we walked back to our car and waited... It was 10 in the morning.</div><hr><div class="bbWrapper">It got dark around 6 in the evening. We were still at exactly the same place. The truck had moved up a bit, but not much.<br>
<br>
We decided that we would go back to the last village and spend the night
 there. We asked the 'Chef du village' and he directed us to Germain, 
the principal of the school. We could camp next to the house of Germain,
 on the school grounds.<br>
<br>
Germain, the principal, was a great guy. Very friendly and concerned 
about everybody. He involved his wife in all the conversations, and she 
too was very friendly to us. Two of their sons were at university, one 
in Kinsasa, the other in Lubumbashi. He was not complaining, but he 
longed back to the olden (colonial) days when roads were functional. 
Apparently until 30 years ago there was a lot of wildlife here. Girafes,
 elephants and lions. His kids had never seen any of these though. All 
of the wildlife had been poached during the different wars.<br>
<br>
Germain's youngest son:<br>
<br>


	
	
		
		
	


	<div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-11-1587032658" data-lb-id="_xfUid-11-1587032658">
		
			<div class="lbContainer-zoomer js-lbImage-_xfUid-11-1587032658" data-src="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo090.jpg" aria-label="Zoom"></div>
		
		<img src="congo_files/kabombo090.jpg" data-url="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo090.jpg" class="bbImage" data-zoom-target="1" alt="" style="">
	</div>

<br>
<br>
Germain's house:<br>
<br>


	
	
		
		
	


	<div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-12-1587032658" data-lb-id="_xfUid-12-1587032658">
		
			<div class="lbContainer-zoomer js-lbImage-_xfUid-12-1587032658" data-src="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo091.jpg" aria-label="Zoom"></div>
		
		<img src="congo_files/kabombo091.jpg" data-url="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo091.jpg" class="bbImage" data-zoom-target="1" alt="" style="">
	</div>

<br>
<br>
Our house:<br>
<br>


	
	
		
		
	


	<div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-13-1587032658" data-lb-id="_xfUid-13-1587032658">
		
			<div class="lbContainer-zoomer js-lbImage-_xfUid-13-1587032658" data-src="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo089.jpg" aria-label="Zoom"></div>
		
		<img src="congo_files/kabombo089.jpg" data-url="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo089.jpg" class="bbImage" data-zoom-target="1" alt="" style="">
	</div></div><hr><div class="bbWrapper">Progress on day 20. Last village before Loange river (don't know the name)<br>
<br>
It's been 25 days since we entered DRC (I started counting the day we left Lubumbashi)<br>
<br>


	
	
		
		
	


	<div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-1-1587032669" data-lb-id="_xfUid-1-1587032669">
		
			<div class="lbContainer-zoomer js-lbImage-_xfUid-1-1587032669" data-src="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/congo_democratic_republic_map-day20.jpg" aria-label="Zoom"></div>
		
		<img src="congo_files/congo_democratic_republic_map-day20.jpg" data-url="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/congo_democratic_republic_map-day20.jpg" class="bbImage" data-zoom-target="1" alt="" style="">
	</div></div><hr><div class="bbWrapper"><b>Day 21</b><br>
<br>
When we got up in the morning we were immediately informed that the big 
truck had still not made it up, so we were not in a hurry.<br>
<br>
We had some more nice talks with Germain and his wife. We wanted to give
 him something as he had been so kind to us. We carry a small photo 
printer, so we decided to take a picture of them and print it off. When 
we told him we wanted to take a picture from him and his familiy he ran 
off quickly..<br>
<br>
Half an hour later he came back, in a costume and shiny shoes. His wife 
was dressed up nicely and even the kids had their Sunday's clothes on. 
They looked fantastic!<br>
<br>


	
	
		
		
	


	<div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-2-1587032669" data-lb-id="_xfUid-2-1587032669">
		
			<div class="lbContainer-zoomer js-lbImage-_xfUid-2-1587032669" data-src="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo093.jpg" aria-label="Zoom"></div>
		
		<img src="congo_files/kabombo093.jpg" data-url="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo093.jpg" class="bbImage" data-zoom-target="1" alt="" style="">
	</div>

<br>
<br>
The (tatty and tired looking) mundele on the right looks fantastic too!  :wink:  <img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" class="smilie smilie--sprite smilie--sprite6" alt=":cool:" title="Cool    :cool:" data-shortname=":cool:"> <br>
<br>


	
	
		
		
	


	<div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-3-1587032669" data-lb-id="_xfUid-3-1587032669">
		
			<div class="lbContainer-zoomer js-lbImage-_xfUid-3-1587032669" data-src="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo096.jpg" aria-label="Zoom"></div>
		
		<img src="congo_files/kabombo096.jpg" data-url="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo096.jpg" class="bbImage" data-zoom-target="1" alt="" style="">
	</div></div><hr><div class="bbWrapper">School grounds usually have a 
lot of kids around. We counted them and stopped at 400. That's more then
 800 prying eyes. Not just kids though, quite a few adults too.<br>
<br>


	
	
		
		
	


	<div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-4-1587032669" data-lb-id="_xfUid-4-1587032669">
		
			<div class="lbContainer-zoomer js-lbImage-_xfUid-4-1587032669" data-src="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/dibaya087.jpg" aria-label="Zoom"></div>
		
		<img src="congo_files/dibaya087.jpg" data-url="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/dibaya087.jpg" class="bbImage" data-zoom-target="1" alt="" style="">
	</div>

<br>
<br>
If I had to choose one picture to describe our Congo trip, that would be the one!<br>
<br>
We decided to drive back to the begining of the descending road, a few 
kilometers out of this village, in the hope to find some peace and 
quiet. That way we would also not loose too much time when the truck 
finally reaches the top.<br>
<br>
When the truck finally reaches the top.<br>
If the truck finally reaches the top.<br>
<br>
With a bit of luck.<br>
<br>
...<br>
<br>
...<br>
<br>
At 6.30 that night we were parked next to Germain's house again. The truck did not make it up today.. <br>
<br>
Germain's wife was so pleased to see us again as she could thank us 
again for the picture we had given her. She had shown it to all her 
friends and was very proud of it.<br>
<br>
Later that night somebody came to tell us that they were going to work 
trough the night to get the truck up.. So maybe we'd be able to move 
again tomorrow?<br>
<br>
With a bit of luck...</div><hr><div class="bbWrapper"><b>Day 22</b><br>
<br>
At 4 that night we were awoken by the sound of a truck engine nearby. 
Finally, the truck had gotten up the hill (it took them 4 days). It was 
still pitch dark, so we tried to sleep a few more hours. But the 
anticipation kept us awake. <br>
<br>
We said our goodbyes to Germain and had a quick talk to the truck 
driver. He was not amused. He started from Kikwit two weeks ago. He said
 if he'd knew about the state of the roads, that he would have never 
taken this job. The GSM operator is paying him well for the transport, 
but he has to pay the local 'helpers' to get him trough here, he will 
have to be lucky to make a profit out of this ride..<br>
<br>
We plummeted down the hill and were grateful that the truck has just 
passed. They had broadened the road and levelled out the deepest ruts. 
We still had a struggle as the trucks massive wheels and ground 
clearance were no match for our little Landcruiser.<br>
<br>
Three hours and 5 kilometers later we arrived at the ferry... <br>
<br>
Oh boy...<br>
<br>


	
	
		
		
	


	<div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-7-1587032669" data-lb-id="_xfUid-7-1587032669">
		
			<div class="lbContainer-zoomer js-lbImage-_xfUid-7-1587032669" data-src="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/dibaya089.jpg" aria-label="Zoom"></div>
		
		<img src="congo_files/dibaya089.jpg" data-url="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/dibaya089.jpg" class="bbImage" data-zoom-target="1" alt="" style="">
	</div></div><hr><div class="bbWrapper">There was a police officer at 
the ferry, and one or two onlookers. But apart from that it was 
surprisingly quiet. The police officer ordered one of the onlookers to 
go and get the captain. We sat down and waited in police guy's little 
hut. He was nervous and young. We joked around a bit with the people who
 were asking for money and cadeaux. Annoying, but not as pushy and 
aggresive as we were used to by now.<br>
<br>
The policy guy then told us we had to register. We did not feel much 
like it, but hey, if he wants to copy over all of our details in his 
little book, why not. When he was done he asked for money as he had 
registered us. We politely told him that registration is not a paying 
service in Congo. It never had been. <br>
<br>
That's not true he said, it is an integral part of his job to ask for 
money from people who pass trough here. We had a chuckle but stayed 
polite and told him that if that is the case, then why is it not in 
writing anywhere.<br>
He then caught us by surprise by claiming it is written in the official 
police handbook! He got up, and got out this little black book. It was 
titled something along the line of "Official guidelines for Police 
officers in the RDC" (not sure about exact title).<br>
<br>
The first chapter were the 'ten commandments' of the police officers, the third item read:<br>
<br>
"Créer une base de données de tous les voyageurs dans votre regio"<br>
<br>
He read it for us and said:<br>
<br>
"Créer une base de donnes de tous les voyageurs dans votre regio"<br>
"Create a base of donations from all travellers in your region"<br>
<br>
We couldn't believe what we heard. Had they really constitutionalized 
corruption? If this was true we would have little choice but to pay.<br>
In disbelief we took the book out of his hands and read the phrase again. Yes, it was really written here...<br>
<br>
It took us a minute before we realised that it was not "donne" 
(pronounced "don", like in Don Quichote) but "donnée" (pronounced 
don-E).<br>
 What was actually written was:<br>
<br>
"Create a database of all travellers in your region"<br>
<br>
We had to try very hard to keep our laughter down. At first we tought he
 just tricked us into it. But that was not the case, he genuinely 
believed it was written that he had to collect money from travellers. He
 had no idea what a database was.</div><hr><div class="bbWrapper">An 
hour later the captain of the ferry arrived. Barthélémy in Ilebo had 
given us a letter for the captain, but as we could not read the letter, 
we did not trust it. We decided to bargain first, and then give the 
letter.<br>
<br>
The captain said he could bring us across for 75$US and 30 liters of diesel. <br>%20
And we had to supply two batteries to get the engine started. <br>
And some oil because the engine was low on oil. <br>
And if possible a diesel filter because his was clogged<br>
<br>
Hmm...<br>
<br>
The diesel we agreed on, 30 liters is a bargain compared to the 
150liters from the last ferry. The price we ridculized and said it was 
waaaay too much. We discussed for half an hour or so, he was friendly 
but stuck to his price. Eventually we 'remembered' the letter. He looked
 happy with what he was reading. Eventually the price got down to 35$US.
 That is still an inflated price, but we agreed. Don't forget that the 
average annual income per capita in 2009 in DRC was estimated at 171$US.
 That's 15$US/month! <br>
<br>
Driving onto the ferry required 4x4 and we banged our gearbox (bashplate
 protected) hard while doing so. We almost cried when we saw the engine 
of this ferry. The 'machine room' was under water. Not really under 
water, it was a mucky oily substance. The engine was leaking badly.<br>
<br>
This ferry had been used to traverse Kabila's army when they marched in 
from the Rwanda border to Kinshasa a few years earlier. The captain told
 us how he was forced to work non-stop for 3 days to get everybody and 
everything across. Every little piece that Kabila's army could use they 
had taken. They left little more then a wreck. <br>
<br>
We had sent out somebody to buy diesel for us, he had to go two villages
 down the road to find some (on his bicycle). In the meantime we tried 
to jumpstart the boat. Our jumpleads were too short to get to it, so we 
took our batteries out (our Landcruiser is 24volt - 2 batteries).<br>
<br>
With our batteries connected the starter motor started turning. Turn 
after turn after turn after turn. Fast at first, but then slower and 
slower. Everybody was dead silent, hoping the engine would start.<br>
<br>
It wouldn't.<br>
<br>
They got to work, and took injectors out and doublechecked various 
parts. Another attempt was made The battery was getting really low and 
just when we thought it was not going to work the big diesel engine 
sprung to life. Hesitant at first, but after a minute or so most of its 
10 cylinders were working. No mufflers ofcourse, this was a LOUD engine!<br>
<br>
The captain's smile was priceless, he was proud of his machine! He gave 
it some trotthle and then the engine sputtered and died. He came down 
and said they had forgotten to purge the fuel system. Air in the system 
and the engine had died. Our batteries were flat now, not enough juice 
left to turn that big starter motor. We tried to start our own engine, 
but even that did not work. Bugger!<br>
<br>
But, no problem, apparantly they had 2 batteries in the village on the 
other side of the river. We could 'rent' them for a small fee. Having no
 other choice we agreed. With a dugout canoe some guys got across in 
search for the batteries. <br>
<br>
An hour later the captain had purged the fuel lines and we had two 'new'
 batteries. They were worthless. They measured 10 volt at most, they 
were not charged! Another solution came up: somebody in the village had a
 solar panel, and we could rent it. They thought it would take 2, maybe 3
 days to get it charged fully.<br>
<br>
Rather not..</div><hr><div class="bbWrapper"><div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-1-1587032679" data-lb-id="_xfUid-1-1587032679">
		
			<div class="lbContainer-zoomer js-lbImage-_xfUid-1-1587032679" data-src="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/dibaya003.jpg" aria-label="Zoom"></div>
		
		<img src="congo_files/dibaya003.jpg" data-url="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/dibaya003.jpg" class="bbImage" data-zoom-target="1" alt="" style="">
	</div>

<br>
<br>
Sitwrap:<br>
<br>
-Our car was sitting on ferry - a ferry we hoped would not sink now<br>
-The ferry was not running<br>
-The ferry did not have batteries<br>
-By attempting to start the ferry, our batteries were now flat<br>
-Our car was not running<br>
-We could not push our car off the ferry as the ramps were damaged.<br>
<br>
If we wanted to charge our batteries, we needed a running engine and a functional alternator.<br>
<br>
We had to push start our Landcruiser, and we had to do it on the few meters of space we had on the ferry.<br>
<br>
Now seems like an appropriate time to talk about our brakes. As we had 
been doing extreme 4x4 work the last 3 weeks in mostly sandy and muddy 
areas, it does not come to a surprise that our brakes took a bit of a 
beating. As a matter of fact, we barely had any functional brakes when 
we left Kananga. And we have no brakes whatsoever since we left Ilebo. 
It's just metal to metal... and that does not brake very well. It hadn't
 bothered me too much until now, the big Landcruiser engine allows for 
great engine breaking and we are not driving any faster then 20km/h 
anyway.<br>
<br>
This made the situation a bit more complicated now..  :roll: <br>
<br>
As you can see on the first picture of this ferry, there is nothing at 
the end of the ferry to stop us from rolling into the fast flowing 
Loange river.<br>
<br>
5 meters. That was the distance we had. The ferry crowd would push us as
 fast as they could, I had to build up enough speed to make the engine 
turn, once started I had to engage reverse gear, let the clutch go - 
gently without stalling the engine or slipping the wheels - and come to a
 halt before rolling into the river. <br>
<br>
5 meters!</div><hr><div class="bbWrapper"><blockquote class="bbCodeBlock bbCodeBlock--expandable bbCodeBlock--quote">
	
		<div class="bbCodeBlock-title">
			
				<a href="file:///forum/goto/post?id=743508" class="bbCodeBlock-sourceJump" data-xf-click="attribution" data-content-selector="#post-743508">RusherRacing said:</a>
			
		</div>
	
	<div class="bbCodeBlock-content">
		
		<div class="bbCodeBlock-expandContent ">
			I have a question for you.  How many languages do you know?
		</div>
		<div class="bbCodeBlock-expandLink"><a>Click to expand...</a></div>
	</div>
</blockquote>Dutch, French and English, that makes 3. Dutch is my mother tongue.<br>
Josephine knows the same languages but German in addition too, and she plans on learning Spanish soon.</div><hr><div class="bbWrapper"><div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-2-1587032679" data-lb-id="_xfUid-2-1587032679">
		
			<div class="lbContainer-zoomer js-lbImage-_xfUid-2-1587032679" data-src="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/dibaya001.jpg" aria-label="Zoom"></div>
		
		<img src="congo_files/dibaya001.jpg" data-url="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/dibaya001.jpg" class="bbImage" data-zoom-target="1" alt="" style="">
	</div>

<br>
<br>
I was a tad bit nervous when the countdown started. I closed the door 
but left the window open and did not wear my seatbelt. Just in case...<br>
<br>
3-2-1 and off we went. I dumped the clutch almost immediately. Way too 
early ofcourse and we came to halt. The engine was not running. On the 
second try I waited 'much' longer, but we did not have enough speed to 
turn the crank. I had about a meter of extra play.<br>
<br>
Third try was a good one. I dumped the clutch and immediately declutched
 again to throw it in reverse and with screeching tyres I reversed again
 just in time. I like a bit of tension, but this was pushing it a bit!<br>
<br>
The next hour we left the engine running with an increased idle to charge the batteries.<br>
<br>


	
	
		
		
	


	<div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-3-1587032679" data-lb-id="_xfUid-3-1587032679">
		
			<div class="lbContainer-zoomer js-lbImage-_xfUid-3-1587032679" data-src="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/dibaya002.jpg" aria-label="Zoom"></div>
		
		<img src="congo_files/dibaya002.jpg" data-url="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/dibaya002.jpg" class="bbImage" data-zoom-target="1" alt="" style="">
	</div>

<br>
<br>
While waiting we had a nice view over the river. At one point we could 
hear drums in the distance, approaching quickly. It was a 'cargo' canoe.
 4 or 5 huge dugout canoes tied together with a huge load on it and 
rowers on bot sides. Somebody was setting the pace with a drum. They 
were travelling downstream and were going really fast. That was one of 
the rare occasion where I saw the Congo I had dreamt about when I was a 
kid.<br>
<br>
We figured that after an hour of charing, the batteries should be loaded
 enough to start the boat engine. So we shut down the engine, took out 
the batteries and hooked it up to the ferry.<br>
<br>
KLIK - KLIK<br>
<br>
Nothing happened...</div><hr><div class="bbWrapper"><blockquote class="bbCodeBlock bbCodeBlock--expandable bbCodeBlock--quote">
	
		<div class="bbCodeBlock-title">
			
				<a href="file:///forum/goto/post?id=743889" class="bbCodeBlock-sourceJump" data-xf-click="attribution" data-content-selector="#post-743889">taco2go said:</a>
			
		</div>
	
	<div class="bbCodeBlock-content">
		
		<div class="bbCodeBlock-expandContent ">
			<div style="text-align: center"><div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-1-1587032690" data-lb-id="_xfUid-1-1587032690">
		
			<div class="lbContainer-zoomer js-lbImage-_xfUid-1-1587032690" data-src="http://i298.photobucket.com/albums/mm264/joashr/Miscellaneous/tintin_02.jpg" aria-label="Zoom"></div>
		
		<img src="congo_files/tintin_02.jpg" data-url="http://i298.photobucket.com/albums/mm264/joashr/Miscellaneous/tintin_02.jpg" class="bbImage" data-zoom-target="1" alt="" style="">
	</div></div>
		</div>
		<div class="bbCodeBlock-expandLink"><a>Click to expand...</a></div>
	</div>
</blockquote>We had this made up for us in Congo :sombrero: It's on our wall in our living room at the moment.<br>
<br>


	
	
		
		
	


	<div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-2-1587032690" data-lb-id="_xfUid-2-1587032690">
		
			<div class="lbContainer-zoomer js-lbImage-_xfUid-2-1587032690" data-src="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/548/aucongo.jpg" aria-label="Zoom"></div>
		
		<img src="congo_files/aucongo.jpg" data-url="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/548/aucongo.jpg" class="bbImage" data-zoom-target="1" alt="" style="">
	</div></div><hr><div class="bbWrapper">I take out the multimeter and it gives funny readings on our batteries. I am starting to fear that they are shot.  :? <br>
<br>
We have been on this ferry for over 4 hours now, and we have not really 
made any progress. Luckily we still have an ace up our sleeves. Our 
Landcruiser is a 24volt version. That means we have 2 batteries for the 
engine. (imagine you arrive here with only one battery?). But we also 
have two auxiliary batteries in the back, to run our fridge. There are a
 couple of reasons we had not used them up until now. Firstly these 
batteries are deep-cycle batteries, they are not made for cranking an 
engine, that could lead to damage. Secondly, to save space in the back I
 put the batteries in a location where they are very difficult to take 
out of. We would have to unload half of the car to get to it, It takes 
at least an hour to take them out. Not a fun thing to do in a car that 
has been standing all day in tropical heat. The idea of exposing all our
 belongings in this crowd is not really a good prospect either.<br>
<br>
But we had no choice now, so we started unloading our car on the ferry. Josephine on guard, me sweating inside.<br>
<br>
An hour later the ferry came to live again. It once again took a lot of 
turns of the startermotor before the engine would go.. these batteries 
too were now completely flat.<br>
<br>
I did not really see anything of our crossing (shame, it was a beautiful
 river) as I was trying to bolt the batteries back into the back of our 
truck.<br>
<br>
At 4 in the afternoon we reached the opposite side. With as much run-up 
as possible they pushed us of the ferry and I managed to get the 
Landcruiser started on my, rough, way down. We were greeted by a 
committee of officials. This was a province border (Kasai/Bandundu).</div><hr><div class="bbWrapper">We
 were invited in the customs hut. It had a little table and a wooden 
bench. And a big book. They registered us, asked for our permit (was ok 
for them), our insurance, etc.. When they were done they asked if we 
"had something for them"<br>
<br>
Time for the usual routine, this gets tiring after a while. So I 
silently count to 3, smile and with a very sweet voice reply that I give
 them my friendship and appreciation and give them a blessing from God..
 And that I am so delighted that such a professional and efficient 
custom service is provided (for free!) by the Congolese government to 
us, humble tourists.<br>
<br>
They ofcourse reply that they want something else. After which I put on a
 very sad face and ask why they do not want my friendship?<br>
<br>
This confuses them. That is the right time to get up, thank and greet 
them with a lot of words (not allowing them to talk back) and walk out 
of the door. <br>
But we were immediately redirected to another hut, the police hut. Same story there, same tricks. <br>
<br>
When we were finally ready to go, the first customs officer comes to me.
 He brings me my hat. I had forgotten it in his hut. These guys aren't 
bad, they do what they have always seen and done (ref. corruption).  We 
are glad we remained friendly with them. We felt very much out of place 
again.<br>
<br>
Slowly we drove off, back into the bush. Our mood changed when we 
realized that we had just left Kasai behind. That was the hardest part 
of our trip. Once we reach Kikwit, we know there is a frequently used 
road to Kinshasa. And from the people on the ferry we learned that there
 is good track - maintained by Belgians - from Dibaya-Lubwe to Kikwit. A
 quick check on the map learned us that we would reach that 'real' road 
soon. Not today, but probably tomorrow, depending on the state of the 
roads.<br>
<br>
The road was pretty bad and the going was slow. But it was not as bad as
 the roads from the last days. Most of the time we were still driving 
off-camber, driving in and out of huge pits, around or over obstacles. 
Slow, first gear movement. But we did not get stuck. It was pretty 
surreal that Josephine and I were having a very animated discussion 
while negotiating these serious obstacles.<br>
<br>
We looked back to everything we have been trough in the last 4 weeks. 
pretty crazy stuff! We have litterally gotten ourselves stuck hundreds 
of times, but we always managed to get us out. We laughed at all the 
nonsense we talked when trying to avoid bribes. We felt really happy 
now, relieved. A great sense of satisfaction came over us. We 
congratulated eachother for helping eachother out when the going got 
tough. We congratulated our Landcruiser for being so tough. <br>
<br>
Then a horrible, dreadful sound.<br>
<br>
*BANG* tak-tak-tak</div><hr><div class="bbWrapper">I stopped 
immediately. I was pretty sure I knew what that sound meant. I turned my
 head towards Josephine and saw a confused, questioning look on her 
face. "What was that?" she asked. I did not say a word and got out of 
the car, looked at the wheels and saw nothing special.I walked to the 
other side of the car and saw this<br>
<br>


	
	
		
		
	


	<div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-3-1587032690" data-lb-id="_xfUid-3-1587032690">
		
			<div class="lbContainer-zoomer js-lbImage-_xfUid-3-1587032690" data-src="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/dibaya004.jpg" aria-label="Zoom"></div>
		
		<img src="congo_files/dibaya004.jpg" data-url="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/dibaya004.jpg" class="bbImage" data-zoom-target="1" alt="" style="">
	</div>

<br>
<br>
Everything seemed to pause for a minute. It was late afternoon and the 
worst heat had worn off, the sounds of the bush were omnipresent. We 
were alone. We were sweating.<br>
<br>
"Josephine, I think we have problem"</div><hr><div class="bbWrapper">All 6 flange bolts had sheared off and the axle had worked its way out. Diff oil was seeping out (and dust in). <br>
<br>
This was not good.<br>
This was not good at all!<br>
<br>
We both sat down for a few minutes and stared at our rear axle. It was a
 sad sight. We are not superstitious at all, but maybe we shouldn't have
 been that positive? This was a scene out of a comic book. It is Donald 
Duck jumping on a bridge, shouting "look how strong this is". And in the
 next scene Donald Duck is seen falling in the water under the 
collapsing bridge.<br>
<br>
A guy on a bicycle stops and looks at the axle. He utters a long "ai ai 
ai ai ai ai" (think "The Gods must be crazy"). This makes us laugh and 
we repeat his "ai ai ai ai ai". He couldn't have expressed our feelings 
better.<br>
The bicycle guy says there is a village 500 meter further down the road.
 Tongue firmly in cheek I ask if there is a Toyota garage in that 
village. He gave me a crazy look. "Il n'y a pas de voiture ici!" - 
"There are no cars here!". <br>
<br>
We have got to do something, so I get out my limited toolbox and start 
removing the rear propshaft and try to close the diff a bit.<br>
<br>


	
	
		
		
	


	<div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-1-1587032701" data-lb-id="_xfUid-1-1587032701">
		
			<div class="lbContainer-zoomer js-lbImage-_xfUid-1-1587032701" data-src="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/dibaya007.jpg" aria-label="Zoom"></div>
		
		<img src="congo_files/dibaya007.jpg" data-url="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/dibaya007.jpg" class="bbImage" data-zoom-target="1" alt="" style="">
	</div>

<br>
<br>


	
	
		
		
	


	<div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-2-1587032701" data-lb-id="_xfUid-2-1587032701">
		
			<div class="lbContainer-zoomer js-lbImage-_xfUid-2-1587032701" data-src="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/dibaya006.jpg" aria-label="Zoom"></div>
		
		<img src="congo_files/dibaya006.jpg" data-url="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/dibaya006.jpg" class="bbImage" data-zoom-target="1" alt="" style="">
	</div>

<br>
<br>
With 4x4 engaged we still have a front wheel drive Landcruiser.</div><hr><div class="bbWrapper">We
 manage to drive to the village, sun is low on the horizon by now so we 
wisely decide to stop here. We need to think about our situation. The 
usual 100 man crowd quickly forms. People here are still annoyingly 
curious, but they do not seem to be as angry as we experienced so much 
in the first part of our trip. Maybe we just get used to it more. Or 
know better how to deal with the situation.<br>
<br>
The chef du village assigns us a spot under a tree and we set up camp. 
Our morale is low, very low! It's been quite the day! First the hairy 
decent down to the Loange river, then the whole debacle to get the ferry
 across, and now with our broken rear axle. <br>
<br>
We are exhausted.<br>
We are dirty. <br>
We are running low on water<br>
<br>
We have barely enough water to cook some pasta, but not enough to 
freshen us up. When asked if they have some water for us, a guy called 
Patty responds cheerfully that their village is blessed as they have a 
fresh water source nearby. That sounds great, I ask if I can fill up one
 of our jerrycans. This is no problem, but he asks if it can wait until 
tomorrow. I push him a bit as we would really like to wash the mud from 
our faces and have enough to drink. He agrees to show me the source.<br>
<br>
So of we go, armed with an empty jerrycan I follow Patty. Out of the 
village. Down a hill. Into a forest. This is a steep track! And muddy! I
 wish I put on my hiking boots for this, I was slipping around on my 
worn crocs. After a 10 minute walk downhill I start questioning Patty 
about this source, I thought it was nearby?!? "Ce n'est pas loin, 
presque là!" - "It's not far, we are almost there".<br>
It is a very narrow track trough the bush. I walk trough spiderwebs a 
few time and mosquitoes are having a ball! Eventually we arrive at a 
murky source down in the valley. I am knackered! 30 minutes down. And 
now we have to go up again. With a full 20 liter jerrycan! I have to 
stop every 10 steps to catch my breath and eventually Patty asks me if 
he should carry the jerrycan. I swallow my pride and my politeness and 
quickly say "Yes! Please!". Patty is fit and strong and jogs up the 
hill. I have problems just keeping up with him.<br>
<br>
We have a nice talk on the way up. A monologue really as I am out of 
breath all the time. He was a nurse and used to work in the first aid 
post of the village. He had been working for free for years as there was
 no budget to pay him. But now they ran out of budget for supplies too. 
The first aid post in the village had been closed last year. If people 
got ill here, they had to travel to Dibaya-Lubwe for even the most basic
 of things.<br>
<br>
An hour and half after we left we returned to the car. I was broken. 
This 5km rough hike was too much for me know. I did not have the 
strength to do these kind of things. The women in the village do this 
twice a day!<br>
<br>
Despite all the worries, I slept well that night!<br>
<br>
Josephine on the other hand.. she did not sleep well. If at all! A 
combination of the poor food and the stress resulted in stumach 
problems... withthe side-effects that we are all familiar with. There 
was a toilet in the village. A hole in the ground with a hut on top of 
it. But it was all the way at the other end of the village. And as a 
bonus there was a huge hairy spider, right next to hole that refused to 
bodge. <br>
<br>
Poor Josephine!  <img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" class="smilie smilie--sprite smilie--sprite15" alt=":cry:" title="Crying    :cry:" data-shortname=":cry:"></div><hr><div class="bbWrapper">Progress afer our 22nd day on the road. 27th day since we entered DRC.<br>
<br>


	
	
		
		
	


	<div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-3-1587032701" data-lb-id="_xfUid-3-1587032701">
		
			<div class="lbContainer-zoomer js-lbImage-_xfUid-3-1587032701" data-src="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/congo_democratic_republic_map-day22.jpg" aria-label="Zoom"></div>
		
		<img src="congo_files/congo_democratic_republic_map-day22.jpg" data-url="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/congo_democratic_republic_map-day22.jpg" class="bbImage" data-zoom-target="1" alt="" style="">
	</div></div><hr><div class="bbWrapper">Last night we had tried sending a
 few SMS messages to our favourite mechanic at home (Hi Pascal!  :wink: 
). The GSM reception was an on-and-off affair and after a few hours of 
trying we managed to get a reply with a few suggestions we could try. 
That would be the first job of the day!<br>
<br>
We had our spectators, but only 40 or 50, which is not all that much. 
Life in the village seemed to continue pretty much as normal. Women were
 grinding the manioc, kids were playing, men were talking in little 
groups.<br>
<br>
Upon closer inspection we saw that the bolts on the flange were broken 
off, leaving all 6 holes filled with the remains of the bolts. We had 
hoped that we could replace some of the bolts with bolts from the front 
axle (a tip from Pascal). But there was no way that was going to work. 
We could weld the axle in place, but there was no welder in this 
village. That would also destroy the axle and the hub, something I did 
not fancy. After all, if we arrive in Kinshasa, we are still 15.000km 
from home!<br>
<br>


	
	
		
		
	


	<div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-4-1587032701" data-lb-id="_xfUid-4-1587032701">
		
			<div class="lbContainer-zoomer js-lbImage-_xfUid-4-1587032701" data-src="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/dibaya005.jpg" aria-label="Zoom"></div>
		
		<img src="congo_files/dibaya005.jpg" data-url="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/dibaya005.jpg" class="bbImage" data-zoom-target="1" alt="" style="">
	</div>

<br>
<br>
The locals suggest we take our entire rear axle out and transport it (by
 bicycle) to Dibaya-lubwe. Fix it there and then transport it back and 
mount it again. That's how the Congolese would handle the situation. We 
had instant flash-backs to the truck that had been waiting for a year 
for its repaired engine. Rather not!<br>
<br>
We could not fix this here. We had to go to Dibaya-Lubwe, the closest 
city. It's not that far, so we decided we would try to get there with 
front wheel drive only. We would take it slowly and carefully!<br>
<br>
We tried sending an SMS home to let them know we broke down. But we had 
no reception at all. Most of the GSM towers here are generator powered 
and only run for a few hours a day (to hard/expensive to get supplies 
in.. and too few paying customers too I presume).<br>
<br>
Our engine did not start. The batteries had not survived the ferry 
ordeal. We had to get a push to get the engine started. We scared the 
people with our insanely loud exhaust (broken off). Children followed 
our badly beaten truck. We were a miserable sight.<br>
<br>
Even before we got back onto the track we got stuck. There was no 
obstacle. Just a bit of soft sand. We needed another push to get going 
again.<br>
<br>
The road is nearly perfect. Nice and flat, a bit sandy. Yet still, we 
get stuck on a 10cm incline. That is ridiculous! With all the weight on 
our rear axle it acts as an anchor. The front wheels on the other hand 
are not that much loaded and battle to grip. A little bit too much 
throttle and they spin.<br>
<br>


	
	
		
		
	


	<div class="lbContainer lbContainer--inline " title="" data-xf-init="lightbox" data-lb-single-image="1" data-lb-container-zoom="1" data-lb-trigger=".js-lbImage-_xfUid-5-1587032701" data-lb-id="_xfUid-5-1587032701">
		
			<div class="lbContainer-zoomer js-lbImage-_xfUid-5-1587032701" data-src="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo075.jpg" aria-label="Zoom"></div>
		
		<img src="congo_files/kabombo075.jpg" data-url="http://radiobaobab.be/assets/etogal/549/kabombo075.jpg" class="bbImage" data-zoom-target="1" alt="" style="">
	</div>

<br>
<br>
Out come the shovels again. For a 10cm hill! This is going to be a long, long day!</div><hr><div class="bbWrapper">This
 went on for hour, after hour, after hour. We would get stuck at the 
most stupid of places. The front axle alone just could not handle the 
weight and bulk of our Landcruiser on these soft sandy roads. We had 
deflated the front tyres and inflated the rear tyres. It helped... but 
not much.<br>
<br>
This was a particularely hot and humid day. We were feeling weak. 
Josephine hadn't slept all night. And my little hike from the last night
 took away a lot of what was left of my strength. I had skipped too many
 meals now, the adrenaline was barely enough to keep me going. To make 
matters worse we entered a forest again and the dreadful ruts 
reappeared. <br>
<br>
This went well for only a very short time. We came to this enormous rut.
 It was uphill so we had to keep our momentum or our front wheels would 
start spinning. <br>
<br>
Drive next to ruts, offcamber on the hill next to it?<br>
Or drive straight into the rut, which would be very bumpy?<br>
<br>
Normally I would have driven next to it. No big deal, it would be scary 
for a minute, but doable. Now with only one opertional axle I wasn't too
 sure. I  hesitated too long. At the last moment I chose to drive next 
to the rut but it was too late. We slid sideways and our rear wheel fell
 into the rut. Our car leaned heavily. Our chassis was grounded. Both 
left wheels were in the air, the rear right wheel was hanging in the 
void of the rut. We only had one wheel on the ground now. The back of 
the car was balancing on the reare axle. We were on the verge of tipping
 over. There would be no sidewall to keep us upright here. If we go 
down, we will end up on our roof.<br>
<br>
We get out and I got really angry! This is not the kind of obstacle were
 we would normally get stuck! I was angry at myself for not taking the 
safer route trough the rut. I was angry at Josephine for... I don't know
 why exactly. I was angry at everybody and everything! I was so 
frustrated!<br>
<br>
Josephine is a hero in these situations and she managed to calm me down.
 We had no other choice but to keep going. This would be one hell of an 
operation to get the car level again.<br>
<br>
We started digging to get some ground under the wheels and make a path to drive out of this.<br>
<br>
By now we were dirty. We hadn't had a decent wash in weeks. The dirt was
 everywhere. It was noon and the sun had no remorse with us. We were 
sweating like pigs. All the dirt that had accumulated on our body now 
turned into mud from our own sweat. With every movement the dirt would 
grind a layer from our skin.<br>
<br>
I stopped digging and got up. I was seeing stars in my eyes. I could 
feel the heat trough my hat. I looked at my muddy arms. I sank trough my
 legs onto my knees. I stayed like that for a few seconds. I wanted to 
get up. *poof* down I went on my back.</div><hr><div class="bbWrapper"><blockquote class="bbCodeBlock bbCodeBlock--expandable bbCodeBlock--quote">
	
		<div class="bbCodeBlock-title">
			
				<a href="file:///forum/goto/post?id=744614" class="bbCodeBlock-sourceJump" data-xf-click="attribution" data-content-selector="#post-744614">RMP&amp;O said:</a>
			
		</div>
	
	<div class="bbCodeBlock-content">
		
		<div class="bbCodeBlock-expandContent ">
			I am curious....why push so hard?
		</div>
		<div class="bbCodeBlock-expandLink"><a>Click to expand...</a></div>
	</div>
</blockquote>Good question. I don't think we were pushing it too hard. We did have a few things in the back of our minds though. <br>
<br>
- We only had a two month visa that started on the day we applied for it
 in Lusaka, Zambia. That meant we had less then three weeks left on that
 visa. That is a long time, but considering the progress we had made 
thus far, it did not give us all that much play. DRC is not the kind of 
country were you want to run into visa issues.<br>
<br>
- We carried food for 30 days. This was our 22nd day so we were starting
 to run low on food. Most of the tasty stuff we had eaten by now. There 
was food around here. Foufou (manioc based dough balls) and if we payed 
to slaughter an animal, we would have meat too. But eating that food 
would increase the risk to get more stomach problems.<br>
<br>
- As you could have seen in the report, we regularly stayed in the same 
place for a day (even after the first day already) just for the reason 
you mention. But a day with 100 people around with no privacy at all 
does not allow for much relaxation. Staying out in the bush we did not 
do for security reasons. The 'gangs' would get us, no matter what. But 
the opportunist would be controlled by the social control in a village.<br>
<br>
- The more breaks you take, the longer 'the misery' takes. I prefer the short pain.<br>
<br>
- At this very moment in the report we made a succesion of mistakes. We 
did not take a break before starting to dig. We got into this mess 
because of a driving error. It just happened...</div><hr><div class="bbWrapper"><blockquote class="bbCodeBlock bbCodeBlock--expandable bbCodeBlock--quote">
	
		<div class="bbCodeBlock-title">
			
				<a href="file:///forum/goto/post?id=744622" class="bbCodeBlock-sourceJump" data-xf-click="attribution" data-content-selector="#post-744622">oryxexpeditions said:</a>
			
		</div>
	
	<div class="bbCodeBlock-content">
		
		<div class="bbCodeBlock-expandContent ">
			I agree. This is supposed to be fun, right?
		</div>
		<div class="bbCodeBlock-expandLink"><a>Click to expand...</a></div>
	</div>
</blockquote>Yes and no. When we decided to do the Congo trip we knew it
 wouldn't all be sunshine and happiness. That is part of the travel 
experience. <br>
<br>
It's a bit like people running marathons. Running 42km can be a painful experience, yet so many people do it for 'fun'.<br>
<br>
But I will be the first to admit that we had gotten a bit more then we had bargained for in Congo!<br>
<br>




<blockquote class="bbCodeBlock bbCodeBlock--expandable bbCodeBlock--quote">
	
	<div class="bbCodeBlock-content">
		
		<div class="bbCodeBlock-expandContent ">
			all the flange bolts shearing off is an unexpected failure. Are these
 naturally weak on LC's, or what? One other thing about a winch is that 
it can save your vehicle a lot of wear and tear.
		</div>
		<div class="bbCodeBlock-expandLink"><a>Click to expand...</a></div>
	</div>
</blockquote>The sun/ring gears in the rear axle are the 'weak' part in 
the driveline as far as my knowledge goes. You are quite right in your 
second statement there, that is why I have a winch on my shopping list 
now ;-) But I'll get back to that later in the report <img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" class="smilie smilie--sprite smilie--sprite1" alt=":)" title="Smile    :)" data-shortname=":)"></div><hr><div class="bbWrapper">I
 had hit a limit. This is exactly the kind of situation where I am at my
 best. Where I am able to keep my cool. Where I am able to remain 
optimistic. I have been able to keep my cool for over 3 weeks now. But I
 lost it here. <br>
<br>
I had a heatstroke too.<br>
<br>
This is where Josephine shines. This time she kept her cool. She put me 
in the shade and prepared me a dehyrdration drink (we have those instant
 solutions in our first aid kit). She found our very last piece of 
chocolate for me. She said the right words to me. Peptalk. Thanks to her
 I quickly came back to reality.<br>
<br>
I wasn't very optimistic though. If somebody popped up here and offered 
to buy our car I wouldn't have hesitated. If we had the possibility to 
take a helicopter and fly out of here, I would have taken the 
opportunity. Both cases were very unlikely to happen!<br>
<br>
While I recovered under a tree Josephine organised our recovery. She 
asked help from somebody that passed by. He wanted money ofcourse, we 
agreed to pay him.. but only afterwards. These guys are strong! It took 
him an hour of digging at an insane rate to make a more-or-less level 
path so we could drive the Landcruiser out of this situation. Driving us
 out of this one was one of the more hair-raising situations, we were 
playing with the laws of physics. The god of gravity had mercy and we 
made it out. This 4x4 work with a front wheel drive car is not easy!<br>
<br>
We paid our helper, we paid him much more then we should have. He was angry and demanded more. We drove off.<br>
<br>
We discussed our situation. Physically I was a wreck. Josephine was not 
feeling too fit either. We knew the 'road' was not that far from here, 
but we would not make it the way we were going now. We agreed we
